<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_100908192">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100908192" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/542037-annalisa" class="left imgcol" title="Annalisa"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1226336540p2/542037.jpg" alt="Annalisa" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/100908192?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 12, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/542037-annalisa" class="user" title="Annalisa">Annalisa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Margaret Atwood fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Annalisa by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana">Tatiana</a>
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/542037-annalisa?shelf=literary" class="actionLinkLite">literary</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/542037-annalisa?shelf=speculative" class="actionLinkLite">speculative</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/542037-annalisa?shelf=sci-fi" class="actionLinkLite">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/542037-annalisa?shelf=apocalypse" class="actionLinkLite">apocalypse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer100908192">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10193752219915309980">I'm struggling to pin a rating on this book. Atwood, as always, is a beautiful writer. The first fifty or so pages I drank up her language, her description and setting. But I have to confess that I didn't like the book. Part of that could be as a parent (of an 8-year-old girl no less) there were parts of Oryx's history that I struggled to read. Child pornography (and abuse) is about the only thing that makes we want to get violent and start castrating guys. After reading that section, I struggle</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10193752219915309980">I'm struggling to pin a rating on this book. Atwood, as always, is a beautiful writer. The first fifty or so pages I drank up her language, her description and setting. But I have to confess that I didn't like the book. Part of that could be as a parent (of an 8-year-old girl no less) there were parts of Oryx's history that I struggled to read. Child pornography (and abuse) is about the only thing that makes we want to get violent and start castrating guys. After reading that section, I struggled to keep my personal feelings out of the story.<br /><br />For much of the book I thought the grittiness of these characters overdone, graphic for the sake of being graphic. By the end of the book I got it. Atwood is comparing the depravity of society with a engineered race that asks the question what is better: innocence without substance or depravity with the complexities of the human mind that include creativity and appreciation for beauty? Does the human race deserve to live with the power we have to destroy our planet and ourselves? When is artificial too much? Image alterations, genetically engineered animals, or even mankind? What are the repercussions of the world we are now creating?<br /><br />I think the concept was interesting and certainly got me thinking. Snowman is left to care for the genetically engineered children of Crake after a lab-produced virus wipes out the human race. Most of the book takes place in disconnected memory, making it slow to read. Even Snowman, while I initially liked him, didn't warrant much sympathy from me. He seemed so emotionally disconnected and didn't value or open up to the myriad of women he slept with. The middle especially dragged for me. I wanted to skip through all the grittiness to the answers Snowman was building up to. And I wanted more from the characters she brushes through. In Margaret Atwood style, we don't get answers as much as questions about society. I was okay with an open ending, but I wanted to know what Crake's intentions had been. We get Snowman's assumptions, but I wasn't a hundred percent sold on his view. I guess that's not the point of the book, though. Does it really matter what intentions people with too much power have when destruction is the same.<br /><br />I'd say the book is a good one for Margaret Atwood fans. I wouldn't say it's as good as Handmaid's Tale, but along the same alley. I may come back and up my rating once the grittiness of the characters is shed and the impression of the novel is left.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10193752219915309980">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer11147110">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16827715469537011912">A mainstream author writing science fiction badly. Basically, tries to have it both ways: referencing real-world, present-day biotechnology without bothering to be accurate about it. I didn't enjoy reading it, and I don't like the implication-- that writing SF just involves throwing terminology around. One wouldn't have much patience for a legal thriller that ignored basic courtroom procedure; one wouldn't have much patience for a medical drama that got human anatomy wrong. I don't have much pat</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16827715469537011912">A mainstream author writing science fiction badly. Basically, tries to have it both ways: referencing real-world, present-day biotechnology without bothering to be accurate about it. I didn't enjoy reading it, and I don't like the implication-- that writing SF just involves throwing terminology around. One wouldn't have much patience for a legal thriller that ignored basic courtroom procedure; one wouldn't have much patience for a medical drama that got human anatomy wrong. I don't have much patience for this.<br /><br />In fairness, the intent may not be science fiction here, but sort of a biotech fantasy (in the sense that <em>Star Wars</em> is fantasy set in space, not that the two works bear any other resemblance.) If so, I still don't think it came off; the story and characters didn't work nearly well enough for me to make up for basic concerns about the premise.</span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13177240873954547696">From my blog: <br /><br />This book was creep-tastically good. Seriously. Reading it disqueted my soul in a way that made me lose my appetite and really hope that this is fiction and not prophecy.<br /><br />Atwood has a knack for writing dystopian novels that are set in the near-enough future to be completely relevant. She basically takes things that we have today, and stretches them into a terrifying future (as she did in the Handmaid's Tale, one of my all-time favorite books). In Oryx and Crake, genetic engineering, bio-form diseases, and ecological disasters have taken their toll on humanity. And as far as I can tell, it takes place around the year 2070. That's within our lifetimes and that's one of the things that makes it so disturbing.<br /><br />It's really hard to write about the plot without giving everything away. It centers on a guy named Jimmy, and vacillates between the time before and after a plague wiped almost all of humanity. I particularly like the scenes after the plague, as Jimmy (or Snowman, as he is called post-annihilation) searches for food and weapons and answers about his two friends, Crake -- a brilliant scientist -- and Oryx -- a former child prostitute and later Jimmy's (and Crake's) lover. Using genetic engineering, Crake has created an entirely new race of people, who are designed to live in harmony without the discord seen in the rest of humanity. These people are quite fascinating -- one of my favorite aspects of this book. <br /><br />Atwood's writing is keen, although not quite as sharp as it was in the Handmaid's Tale. I wish that Oryx's character had been developed a little further, although she was the only character who fell a little flat. But her imagined world is very tangible and populated with things we have now, making it all too real. Atwood succeeded in most aspects of her novel, and it leaves me wanting to read more of her work!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13177240873954547696">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4222053259508519975">I have some thoughts about Oryx, as I too noticed that she wasn't very developed. However, the novel is told from Snowman's point of view, and through</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4222053259508519975">I have some thoughts about Oryx, as I too noticed that she wasn't very developed. However, the novel is told from Snowman's point of view, and throughout the novel it's clear that he (as well as Crake and society as a whole) is a misogynist. The interactions he has with Oryx that Atwood chooses to describe often involve him trying to find out more about her past, and her resisting. Rather than encounter has as an actual human being right in front of him, he wants her to function as a victim for him to save, thus pinning her down as an object with certain characteristics rather than as a human being with her own agency. As I recall, more than once she says to him "Jimmy, that's not me," but he always ignores that. Anyway, I was thinking that the character might have been a bit underdeveloped because Jimmy could only see her as a thing, so the reader is required to read more into the brief interactions we have with her to flesh her out more.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_25499766">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/25499766" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1219253-amanda" class="left imgcol" title="Amanda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1411956191p2/1219253.jpg" alt="Amanda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/25499766?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 14, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1219253-amanda" class="user" title="Amanda">Amanda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Speculative Fiction/Dystopian Fans</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1219253-amanda?shelf=blog" class="actionLinkLite">blog</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer25499766">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13074023368383762378">I've read a few of Margaret Atwood's poems and short stories, but this is my first official Atwood novel (I now imagine a collective gasp from all the hardcore <em>The Handmaid's Tale</em> fans . . . not to worry, that's on my reading list as I've already procured a copy). I've always heard people rave about how wonderful Atwood is and I can now say that I finally know what all the fuss is about. <br /><br />A dystopian novel, <em>Oryx and Crake</em> is set in a not-so-distant apocalyptic future in which mankind has been era</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13074023368383762378">I've read a few of Margaret Atwood's poems and short stories, but this is my first official Atwood novel (I now imagine a collective gasp from all the hardcore <em>The Handmaid's Tale</em> fans . . . not to worry, that's on my reading list as I've already procured a copy). I've always heard people rave about how wonderful Atwood is and I can now say that I finally know what all the fuss is about. <br /><br />A dystopian novel, <em>Oryx and Crake</em> is set in a not-so-distant apocalyptic future in which mankind has been eradicated by the jealousy of one man, Crake. We soon learn that Crake was the childhood best friend of the narrator, Jimmy (aka Snowman). While I don't want to give too much away regarding the how and why of this catastrophic event, it is Jimmy's fate to live on so that he can care for the "Crakers," a race of genetically altered human beings that have been modified by Crake to be docile herbivores, existing like animals in a natural habitat and never plagued by petty human concerns. <br /><br />For all the inventiveness of Atwood's imagined future (video games, the increasing power of the Internet, genetic splicing, biotechnology, etc.), this is not so much a story about the end of humanity as it is a story about Jimmy, his life and the destructive love that brought about his castaway status in a future he couldn't have imagined. Atwood doesn't offer answers and is never didactic--she simply tells a story and that story speaks volumes about what may be the future of humanity.<br /><br />Cross posted at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://viiamanda.blogspot.com/">This Insignificant Cinder</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13074023368383762378">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_66936455">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/66936455" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2613919-jason" class="left imgcol" title="Jason"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451696976p2/2613919.jpg" alt="Jason" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/66936455?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 04, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2613919-jason" class="user" title="Jason">Jason</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5088200-oryx-and-crake" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2613919-jason?shelf=e-books" class="actionLinkLite">e-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2613919-jason?shelf=read-2009" class="actionLinkLite">read-2009</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2613919-jason?shelf=reread" class="actionLinkLite">reread</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2613919-jason?shelf=read-2014" class="actionLinkLite">read-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer66936455">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10040555061428198606">5 Stars<br /><br />Oryx and Crake really needed a second read through for me to appreciate the quality of this story. Atwood has created a well thought out, well written, and a detailed post apocalyptic novel that others will be measured against. The style of this book along with the writing is what sets it apart. This is not a heavy action novel, nor is the world very different from countless other fictional ones. The characters are very identifiable but not really remarkable. The cause of the worlds end i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10040555061428198606">5 Stars<br /><br />Oryx and Crake really needed a second read through for me to appreciate the quality of this story. Atwood has created a well thought out, well written, and a detailed post apocalyptic novel that others will be measured against. The style of this book along with the writing is what sets it apart. This is not a heavy action novel, nor is the world very different from countless other fictional ones. The characters are very identifiable but not really remarkable. The cause of the worlds end is often the subject but it is not the star or the center of this book.<br /><br />Atwood uses a technique of world building through bouncing the story line back and forth from the present back to the past. This really works at keeping the reader interested and the story made more complete. Atwood then adds to her craft by featuring two main protagonist women. She sets them apart by telling one in the first person always, and the other in the third.<br /><br />Although I really liked this book the first time I read it, I loved it the second time through. <br /><br />A sample of her themes and her writing:<br /><br />“”…Jimmy, look at it realistically. You can’t couple a minimum access to food with an expanding population indefinitely. Homo Sapiens doesn't seem able to cut himself off at the supply end. He’s one of the few species that doesn't limit reproduction in the face of dwindling resources. In other words - the less we eat, the more we fuck.””<br /><br /><br />highly recommended!!!<br /><br />I just started my reread. <br /><br /><br /><br />Oryx and Crake was a mixed bag for me. For a novel it was just OK, the protagonist is likeable and the supporting cast not bad either. The world is well detailed and described, and it is very much like our own. The story is well paced but suffers to me from an ending that just does not live up to the tension built within. <br /> Oryx and Crake is successful on a higher intellectual level when the story is done and you have time to digest just what it was all for. The “What If” scenarios of this book are so plausible that I have had further conversations with my wife about them. I do recommend this book for any searching for a satisfying, realistic, fictional apocalypse story.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10040555061428198606">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_11092043">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11092043" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/262254-greg" class="left imgcol" title="Greg"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1273956732p2/262254.jpg" alt="Greg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/11092043?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 28, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/262254-greg" class="user" title="Greg">Greg</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/46756.Oryx_and_Crake" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780385721677" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Greg by: </span>
      Aerin
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/262254-greg?shelf=speculative-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">speculative-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer11092043">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15209592239259985859">I've read a lot of dystopian future books, and this was by far the most unsettling of the bunch. I think one of the reasons I enjoy reading these types of novels is the plausibility that surrounds the settings they take place in, and Oryx and Crake struck me as far more plausible than any of the others I've read. Partly because it doesn't take place all that far in the future, and partly because most of technology that the story depends on already exists, or is right around the corner. I think m</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15209592239259985859">I've read a lot of dystopian future books, and this was by far the most unsettling of the bunch. I think one of the reasons I enjoy reading these types of novels is the plausibility that surrounds the settings they take place in, and Oryx and Crake struck me as far more plausible than any of the others I've read. Partly because it doesn't take place all that far in the future, and partly because most of technology that the story depends on already exists, or is right around the corner. I think much of what keeps us (as in society) from playing around in these areas is taboo, and that won't last forever. <br /><br />The field of bioethics (or neuroethics) is becoming more relevant than ever and will have to seriously address questions that many of us don't want to think about. Because the scary thing is...we're already on our way to the world Atwood describes in this book. We're already growing our livestock to produce more meat, so much so that the chickens produced from this are too heavy to support their own body weight. How long before we have the power to modify these animals as we please? Neuroscience is discovering more and more about the nature of disorders and diseases and there are few that would argue against our pursuit of curing Alzheimer's and MS and schizophrenia if we have the chance. But it's just a small hop, skip, and a jump from removing negative qualities to boosting positive ones. Which in itself isn't bad, so much as the realities of availability and accessibility. <br /><br />I think the scariest part of all though comes from the fact that the character in the story who is the cause of our post apocalyptic future isn't an evil lunatic, but a troubled genius, depressed with humanity and what we do to ourselves and our planet. He does what he does not because he wants to destroy, but because he wants to rebuild. Or at least preserve. <br /><br />What really hooked me about this book was the myriad of details Atwood was able to stick in about the world the story takes place in. From the corporate compounds that separated the privileged employees from the rest of the world, the rampant consumerism surrounding our biological achievements, to the effects of global warming insinuating itself into our every day lives, Atwood creates a rich, and terrifying future.<br /><br />My only disappointment was lackluster ending. It seemed like Atwood told the story she wanted to tell and then ran out of ideas of how to wrap it all up properly. Or maybe that was the point...</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15209592239259985859">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_230264623">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/230264623" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey" class="left imgcol" title="Aubrey"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1440305114p2/4119460.jpg" alt="Aubrey" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/230264623?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 30, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey" class="user" title="Aubrey">Aubrey</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/46756.Oryx_and_Crake" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780385721677" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=2-star" class="actionLinkLite">2-star</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check" class="actionLinkLite">reality-check</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=prince-asturias" class="actionLinkLite">prince-asturias</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand" class="actionLinkLite">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=women-s-prize" class="actionLinkLite">women-s-prize</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=virago" class="actionLinkLite">virago</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads" class="actionLinkLite">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2015" class="actionLinkLite">r-2015</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=sow15" class="actionLinkLite">sow15</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read" class="actionLinkLite">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all" class="actionLinkLite">antidote-think-twice-all</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer230264623">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11423128077640117846">Spoilers, maybe. I don't know, it's hard to tell with this one.<br /><br />A while back I was enamored with this show called Sherlock that, for all I know, is still airing strong on the BBC network. This was during my Bioengineering days, the pinnacle of my proneness to eugenics flavored gobbledygook of the "high functioning sociopath" sort and all that sterile nonsense, so I can't give my younger self too much crap for being trained to confuse ethics with money and humanity with science. These days, howeve</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11423128077640117846">Spoilers, maybe. I don't know, it's hard to tell with this one.<br /><br />A while back I was enamored with this show called Sherlock that, for all I know, is still airing strong on the BBC network. This was during my Bioengineering days, the pinnacle of my proneness to eugenics flavored gobbledygook of the "high functioning sociopath" sort and all that sterile nonsense, so I can't give my younger self too much crap for being trained to confuse ethics with money and humanity with science. These days, however, are another matter entirely cause seriously, I don't care if you favor one over the other. Just don't mishmash piss poor versions into each other and expect me to thank you, cause the only way I'll suspend disbelief over war crime scale violations is if the realities of chemicals and gravity and extinction are taken into account. If you can't even manage that, what was all that highfalutin self-serving nonsense done for in the first place?<br /><br />If Crake's the "high functioning sociopath" (so much shit wrong with that hierarchical ableist horseshit I won't even start), Jimmy's the everyman, and let me tell you: if you're going to trap me in the head of white het cis male à la Pip and Theo and Philip and Nick Carraway and god knows how many others which litter the so called canon, you better make them <em>interesting</em>. Phase out the mother if you must, but if it's a feminine dribble fall out with nary a hint of impact beyond some badly handled Oedpius complex, <em>boring</em>. Give the boy an artistic bent, but if it doesn't get farther than a lexiconless word bank and an intriguing computer game that barely gets five paragraphs to its narrative credit, <em>dull</em>. Have pages and pages of sex and porn and all sorts of erotic fancies, but if you can't even grant me a single page of somewhat sensual <em>explication</em> of all this riotous libido that is supposedly going on, what is the actual point of having that be the only thing this close third-person ever commits to? That and the stereotypes, which just made me want to go back to <a title="The Windup Girl by Paolo Bacigalupi" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6597651.The_Windup_Girl">The Windup Girl</a> and facepalm forever. There's nothing more boring than futuristic dystopias that act like mainstream news is actually reality.<br /><br />All that previous would've been slightly tolerable had the world made up for the narrator's craptastic view of it, but here's the rub. Crake, this typical I-am-beyond-the-laws-of-you-puny-humans-muahahaha that is the focal point of many a movie and TV show, is supposed to be the tip top superman genius, yes? Well, riddle me this: why is it that, in this futuristic dystopia of spliced together ingeniousness and tech of the super awesome, the world has as a whole drop kicked Darwin out the window? If Crake's so smart, how come he obsessed so much about the small details of rape and violence and murder in the human species when the extremely simple principle of <em>natural selection</em> was going to burn all his dreams to the ground if he didn't watch himself? If he knew everything that there was to know about the science of survival and then some, if he was so caught up in his little extinction game that he knew exactly what needed to be done to ensure humans got past the ultimate bottleneck, <em>why is perfection still a consideration in his game plan</em>?<br /><br />I've forgotten most of my biochem and my astrophysics and my ecological fundamentals, but here's the thing about survival that's very easy to get: the world doesn't care about your single precious perfect pack of babies made through the most complicated means of gene insertion and cross species contamination. If they're perfect, aka they adhere to a very limited selection of standards of physicality and biological intrepidness, what kills one is going to kill them all. Slight change in the average temperature to ten or so degrees below the accustomed norm? Gone. Meteor strike? Kaboom. Some disease surmounts all those carefully planned immunity and brings them all to their melting knees? I thought something was up when the presumption was put forward that was it even <em>possible</em> to cure everything, as if the sheer numbers of viral pools and bacterial counts involved in their own hyperspeed evolution suddenly didn't matter anymore, but I never thought that the character who was supposed to be the smartest in the world evar would completely ignore the tenets of extinction he was so enamored with and think that perfection was the way to go. Cause see, perfection does not <em>survive</em>. Variability does. Human definitions of ugliness and grossness and less than aesthetically pleasing body forms and biological functions do, cause when the next almighty bottleneck hits, it doesn't matter what one set of adaptations is like. If you don't have a myriad combo that can mate and mutate and maybe hopefully get those fertile two who hit the survivability jackpot, your work's going to melt into a bloody slagheap just like the rest of them. All for your precious "perfection" in a world whose aesthetic standards were supposed to have been shot to hell.<br /><br />Here's the thing about genocide: it fucks over our species' genetic variability pools like nothing else. So, you want to orchestrate your own biblical flood and replenish accordingly? You better have one hundred, two hundred, hell, a million variable genomes that are similar enough to reproduce fertile offspring, variable enough to breed through whatever nuclear fallout or cockroach invasion is in this planet's future. Else, this happy go lucky Eden of biologically ethical humanity is going to sink, and all your quibbling about theology and sexual assault will be so much dust in the wind. In the midst of Atwood's scientific detailing, I saw neither hide nor hair of this natural selection insurance: just a bunch of talk about "they come in all colors!!!!" that reminds me more of colorblind ideologies of the liberal sort than any sort of ecological infrastructure. If you're going to bore me out of my gourd in all the non-science aspects of a narrative, you <em>cannot</em> mess up this badly. It's not technical, or specific, or something that requires a four year degree. What it is is a simple matter of the number of chances you're allotted ground through an unknown number of scenarios for as long as it's feasible via luck and circumstance, and the only way to stop this is to halt time entirely. In short, my ethics serves a purpose other than affirmative action guilt or whatever else it's chocked up as by others. You keep putting the species through bottlenecks in the effort of supremacy, we're all gonna die.<br /><br />At the tail end of Summer of Women, I guess I can be gratified by a woman being rewarded as much for shoddy work as a man would be. Should I have been more wary of the number of ratings and/or interested friends? Maybe. Should I take a break from dystopias for a decade or so? Probably. <a title="Grotesque by Natsuo Kirino" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25366.Grotesque">Grotesque</a> wiped the floor with this in terms of rape culture and contemporary noir and sociopolitical analysis, and seeing how both are pop fiction, you can't say I'm being half-handedly elitist, or, god forbid, <em>mean</em>. It's just aggravating when writers think any future they conjure up in their heads is worse than the realities of today.<br /><br /><br />P.S. That second star is for the concept of Blood and Roses game. It needs a lot of tweaking and social justice expansion, but it's a grand start. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11423128077640117846">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_587637390">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/587637390" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2457095-apatt" class="left imgcol" title="Apatt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" alt="Apatt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/587637390?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 18, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2457095-apatt" class="user" title="Apatt">Apatt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/46756.Oryx_and_Crake" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780385721677" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer587637390">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14211259815468473709"><em>Dear Ms. Atwood,<br /><br />In the unlikely event that you are reading this I want you to know that all is forgiven.</em><br /><br />"Whatchutalkinbout?" I hear you say, or perhaps I don't because it is fairly common knowledge that Margaret Atwood does not consider her sf books "science fiction", "speculative fiction" she allows but "science fiction" is a definite no-no because (according to her) it is full of talking squid-like aliens thingies (If this is news to you, you may want to look up her <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Margaret_Atwood#Atwood_and_science_fiction">Wikipedia entry</a> and other s</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14211259815468473709"><em>Dear Ms. Atwood,<br /><br />In the unlikely event that you are reading this I want you to know that all is forgiven.</em><br /><br />"Whatchutalkinbout?" I hear you say, or perhaps I don't because it is fairly common knowledge that Margaret Atwood does not consider her sf books "science fiction", "speculative fiction" she allows but "science fiction" is a definite no-no because (according to her) it is full of talking squid-like aliens thingies (If this is news to you, you may want to look up her <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Margaret_Atwood#Atwood_and_science_fiction">Wikipedia entry</a> and other sources). Being a sci-fi fanatic I could not bring myself to read one of her books for the longest time because of this attitude.<br /><br />My loss.<br /><br />Now the way I figure it, when an author is <em>this</em> good, you got to forgive them their eccentricity. In all fairness it is her prerogative, and if she wants to call <em>Oryx and Crake</em> or <a title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale">The Handmaid's Tale</a> cookbooks or a vampire romances it doesn't make the books any less brilliant.<br /><br />I quite like <a title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale">The Handmaid's Tale</a> but I did not feel like I was in her target readership and it is just another pretty good book among many good books that I read last year. <em>Oryx and Crake</em> on the other hand is just hits the spot! The story, the characters, the world building, the themes, and the prose combine together into an extraordinary novel, not a single misstep. I was going to complain about the frequent Present day to flashback structure but I became accustomed to and fascinated by both time lines.<br /><br /><em>Oryx and Crake</em> is a dystopian <em>and</em> a post-apocalypse novel, these two subgenres of sf are often misunderstood to be interchangeable terms. The fact is in a dystopian world society still functions in horribly messed up in some "you would not want to live there" ways but it is still a society. In a post-apocalypse setting society has already fallen and there is no governing body of any kind. The "present day" framing story is set after the apocalypse has already occurred and the flashbacks gradually fill in the details of how this came about. In the near future dystopia where the flashback sections of the novel is set the world is dominated by multinationals and society is becoming increasingly depraved, food and natural resources are scarce. Scientists see genetic engineering and biotechnology as the answer to all of society's woes. <em>Oryx and Crake</em> is the story of what happen when genetic engineering is allowed to develop is unhindered as long as there is profit to be made. The eponymous Oryx and Crake are main characters but the actual protagonist is Jimmy who later renames himself Snowman, possibly the last human being alive, excluding a group of posthumans / subhumans whose welfare and survival Jimmy has taken responsibility for.<br /><br />This review is already too long for its own good, I could go on and on about the relentlessly inventive elements of this book and how it eventually touches at the emotional core and tugs at the heartstrings. It leaves me in an unusually pensive frame of mind. Any book that can do that deserves the highest recommendation.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14211259815468473709">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_81118569">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/81118569" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/111921-madeline" class="left imgcol" title="Madeline"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1220803522p2/111921.jpg" alt="Madeline" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/81118569?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 15, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/111921-madeline" class="user" title="Madeline">Madeline</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/46756.Oryx_and_Crake" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780385721677" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer81118569">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7356169055708654541">Nobody can write dystopia like Atwood. Depending on your preference, that's either a good thing or a bad thing. For me, it's a very good thing. <br /><br />If we think of <a title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale">The Handmaid's Tale</a> as a religious dystopia, then this book is a scientific dystopia. I'm afraid to describe the plot in too much detail, because I'm afraid to give anything away, but here's the basic outline - the story is narrated by a man who calls himself Snowman. He appears to be the only human survivor of a huge disaster, although th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7356169055708654541">Nobody can write dystopia like Atwood. Depending on your preference, that's either a good thing or a bad thing. For me, it's a very good thing. <br /><br />If we think of <a title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale">The Handmaid's Tale</a> as a religious dystopia, then this book is a scientific dystopia. I'm afraid to describe the plot in too much detail, because I'm afraid to give anything away, but here's the basic outline - the story is narrated by a man who calls himself Snowman. He appears to be the only human survivor of a huge disaster, although there is another group of people living near him, whom he calls the Children of Crake. Snowman is their prophet, their connection to their mysterious creator Crake and his counterpart, Oryx, who Snowman says created the animals. He tells the Children of Crake stories about their creator, and answers their questions about life and death with made-up stories that would be funny if they weren't so creepily similar to real religious stories and mythology. In between Snowman's interactions with the Crakers, we get flashbacks to his childhood, when he was known as Jimmy and lived on a compound sealed off from the disease-ridden "pleeblands" beyond. He went to school with Crake, and we follow the two of them through high school and into college, while Crake is quietly working on his own plan to change the world. <br /><br />I made sort of a mess of the plot there, so don't worry if you're confused. Atwood explains it much better, believe me. <br /><br />The little details of the world Atwood created were the best part of this book for me. She spends the better part of a chapter describing the types of computer games Crake and Jimmy played when they were teenagers, and it's kind of amazing:<br />"Blood and Roses was a trading game, along the lines of Monopoly. The Blood side played with human atrocities for the counters, atrocities on a large scale: individual rapes and murders didn't count, there had to have been a large number of people wiped out. Massacres, genocides, that sort of thing. The Roses side played with human achievements. Artworks, scientific breakthroughs, stellar works of architecture, helpful inventions. <em>Monuments to the soul's magnificence</em>, they were called in the game. There were sidebar buttons, so that if you didn't know what <em>Crime and Punishment</em> was, or the Theory of Relativity, or the Trail of Tears, or <em>Madame Bovary</em>, or the Hundred Years' War, or the <em>Flight into Egypt</em>, you could double-click and get an illustrated rundown, in two choices: R for children, PON for Profanity, Obscenity, and Nudity. That was the thing about history, said Crake: it had lots of all three." <br /><br />I'll give the last word to Atwood herself, who writes in an addendum at the back of the book, "Like <em>The Handmaid's Tale</em>, <em>Oryx and Crake</em> is a speculative fiction, not science fiction proper. It contains no intergalactic space travel, no teleportation, no Martians. As with <em>The Handmaid's Tale</em>, it invents nothing we haven't already invented or started to invent. Every novel begins with a <em>what if</em>, and then sets forth its axioms. The <em>what if</em> of <em>Oryx and Crake</em> is simply, <em>What if we continue down the road we're already one?</em> How slippery is the slope? What are our saving graces? Who's got the will to stop us?"</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7356169055708654541">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_499028533">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/499028533" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/8802423-david-sven" class="left imgcol" title="David Sven"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1402220807p2/8802423.jpg" alt="David Sven" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/499028533?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 24, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8802423-david-sven" class="user" title="David Sven">David Sven</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5088200-oryx-and-crake" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/8802423-david-sven?shelf=sci-fi" class="actionLinkLite">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer499028533">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6625356734849700861">Clever, thought provoking writing that raises the question of what it means to be human. Through the eyes of the main protagonist, Jimmy, AKA “Snowman,” we are introduced to a post apocalyptic future where humanity has been wiped out, except for “Snowman” and the “Children of Crake,” ie re-engineered humans – if they can be called human. Who Crake is and who Snowman is, and what happened to the world is left to the reader to piece together as we go back into Jimmy’s past to a world before the fa</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6625356734849700861">Clever, thought provoking writing that raises the question of what it means to be human. Through the eyes of the main protagonist, Jimmy, AKA “Snowman,” we are introduced to a post apocalyptic future where humanity has been wiped out, except for “Snowman” and the “Children of Crake,” ie re-engineered humans – if they can be called human. Who Crake is and who Snowman is, and what happened to the world is left to the reader to piece together as we go back into Jimmy’s past to a world before the fall of the human race.<br /><br />Before the fall, the world is descending into chaos as population growth outstrips food supply and Corporations compete to capitalize on increased demand for "goods" through advances in bioengineering. Both food and organs are grown in biological farms. Companies seek an edge over each other by engaging in bio terrorism. Nasty biological agents capable of turning a human being into soup in minutes are being played with. Safety procedures are developed, not to protect humans from being infected, but to protect the corporations’ patented biological products.<br /><br />Humanity is divided into the "haves" and "have nots" as companies occupy the safe and affluent “Compounds” while everyone else is left in the chaos that is the “Pleeblands.” Life is cheap. Children are considered commodities to be exploited and sold and we hear some of their stories, told with a casual callousness that is at once heart breaking and confronting.<br /><br />The most chilling aspect of this book is that the culture and attitudes presented are not that far removed from where we are today. Particularly in the West, we pay lip service to child protection, and yet at the same time we are a major consumer of child porn and child exploitation. Even within the boundaries of legality, children are still becoming increasingly sexualized in advertising, and in our pageants and even some politicians are pushing for the legal age of consent to be dropped.<br />Why then should we expect these children – the next generation of politicians, scientists, corporate decision makers – why should we expect they will make moral ethical decisions when it comes to scientific fields like genetics, cloning, or biological warfare?<br /><br />And where does it end? This book takes a look at one possible ending. If we had the science to re-engineer human beings, how long before we would justify crossing that ethical boundary? One generation? Two?<br /><br />In this book we get a closeup, and often uncomfortable look at the human condition as Atwood rides current attitudes in science and modern western culture through to their ultimate logical post apocalyptic conclusion.<br /><br />Engaging reading. <br /><br /><br />4 stars<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6625356734849700861">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer722971990">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4663148304286040440">Dear students from the year 2113 or beyond,<br /><br />I write this review ten years after the book was released and a century before you are going to read it.<br />If you are there, then we didn't fuck up in one way or another. In what context you're reading this review, I can only guess.<br />Are you educated people are living in compounds now as the novel suggested? Are the social canyons between the haves and the have nots so huge that you had to split society as it was done in the novel?<br /><br />And what about the way of e</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4663148304286040440">Dear students from the year 2113 or beyond,<br /><br />I write this review ten years after the book was released and a century before you are going to read it.<br />If you are there, then we didn't fuck up in one way or another. In what context you're reading this review, I can only guess.<br />Are you educated people are living in compounds now as the novel suggested? Are the social canyons between the haves and the have nots so huge that you had to split society as it was done in the novel?<br /><br />And what about the way of entertaining yourself? Has our trend from the book come true? Are our voyeuristic behaviours borderless? Do you watch people's deaths, their intimacy and their last threads of dignity on tv, while you're eating artificial food products?<br />And is reading this review part of your education or a voyeuristic escapade into the beginning of our millenium. Are you looking me up on a social network right now. Do you know how I died? Did I have privacy in the end or are you entertaining yourself right now by watching me die in a loop over and over again?<br />We're the first generation that can really talk to you, so let's talk. What did we do with the world and what do you do with it right now?<br /><br />Are we on the road to becoming the society of Oryx and Crake or did we/you somehow do something differnt?<br />Do you read this review, because the book teaches you something about the dangers of your past, or do you read it as a literary project?<br />Cause that is the main focus of the book. If you're into thought-provoking sandbox scenarios, then this book is clearly a five star read. It encouraged me to talk to you, people who were born, after I died, so it's great in one way.<br />But on the other hand the story itself that comes with all the thought-provoking ideas is fairly basic, sometimes even a bit boring. So, why do you read this? For history class? For english? <br />The success of the book for you mainly depends on your approach.<br />So, I rated it 3 stars, cause my approach was wrong in the first place. I read it for entertainment.<br /><br />So, what are you doing now? Do you get your A, cause you found this remnant from the past talk to you from the bowels of the internet?<br />Do you shake your head about the people back in my time and what they thought might happen? It's the fucking 22nd century, so where are you? In a bright new future on in a toxic wasteland. Did society crake it, or are their still little Jimmy's out there, like me? </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_333318515">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/333318515" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9653496-hanne" class="left imgcol" title="Hanne"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1381183161p2/9653496.jpg" alt="Hanne" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/333318515?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 26, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9653496-hanne" class="user" title="Hanne">Hanne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/856096.Oryx_and_Crake" title="Paperback 9781844080564" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/9653496-hanne?shelf=favourites" class="actionLinkLite">favourites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9653496-hanne?shelf=post-apocalypse-or-dystopia" class="actionLinkLite">post-apocalypse-or-dystopia</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9653496-hanne?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer333318515">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8740590956834477872">I first read this book back in 2006 when post-apocalyptic books weren't all the rage yet. To me, this book was jawdropping, I hadn't read anything even remotely like it and it quickly earned a spot on my favourites shelf.<br />Now, six years and many post-apocalyptic books later, I revisited it, and it still hasn't lost its brilliance in many ways. <br /><br />This is not a fast paced book, there is plenty of internalizing and describing. It brings up a lot of ethical issues about where science is heading. Where</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8740590956834477872">I first read this book back in 2006 when post-apocalyptic books weren't all the rage yet. To me, this book was jawdropping, I hadn't read anything even remotely like it and it quickly earned a spot on my favourites shelf.<br />Now, six years and many post-apocalyptic books later, I revisited it, and it still hasn't lost its brilliance in many ways. <br /><br />This is not a fast paced book, there is plenty of internalizing and describing. It brings up a lot of ethical issues about where science is heading. Where is the line? When is too much too much? If your intentions are good, can you do it? Can you do the exact same thing, if your intentions are not so good? And who decides the difference?<br /><br /><br /><em>“These things sneak up on him for no reason, these flashes of irrational happiness. It's probably a vitamin deficiency.”</em><br /><br />There are many things wrong with the world Atwood paints here, and the vitamin deficiencies are the least of their problems. And yet, what Atwoord does really well is bringing up all this issues without becoming a heavy difficult read and leaving a lot of room for fun speculation. <br />Above anything else, I think this book is just very cleverly done. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8740590956834477872">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy" class="user" title="Jenny (Reading Envy)">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17054363020323811655">This book stands up to multiple readings, and I'm not sure why I don't have a review of it, but stay tuned. This is my fifth read. I re-read the trilogy to prepare for a discussion on <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.sffaudio.com/?p=49462">SFF Audio</a>, which actually answered a lot of the questions I've had in the five reads of this book.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[<br />This time, I read it with the idea of trying to figure out the whys. QUotations below figure into it, I'll flesh it out on another day.<br /><br />"That was the trouble with Blood and Roses: it was easier to remember the Blood stuff. The other trouble was that the Blood player usually won, but winning meant you inherited a wasteland. This was the point of the game, said Crake, when Jimmy complained." (80)<br /><br />"Crake grinned a lot when watching this site. [nitee-nite.com, an assisted suicide site] For some reason he found it hilarious, whereas Jimmy did not. He couldn't imagine doing such a thing himself, unlike Crake, who said it showed flair to know when you'd had enough." (84)<br /><br />"Toast is me. I am toast." &lt;-- retelling history since it won't make sense (98)<br /><br />"All it takes," said Crake, "is the elimination of one generation. One generation of anything. Beetles, trees, microbes, scientists, speakers of French, whatever. Break the link in time between one generation and the next, and it's game over forever." (223)<br /><br />"Had Oryx loved him, had she loved him not, did Crake know about them, how much did he know, when did he know it, was he spying on them all along? Did he set up the grand finale as an assisted suicide, had he intended to have Jimmy shoot him because he knew what would happen next and he didn't deign to stick around to watch the results of what he'd done?<br /><br />Or did he know he wouldn't be able to withhold the formula for the vaccine, once the CorpSeCorps got to work on him? How long had he been planning this? ... With so much at stake, was he afraid of failure, of being just one more incompetent nihilist? Or was he tormented by jealousy, was he addled by love, was it revenge, did he just want Jimmy to put him out of his misery? Had he been a lunatic or an intellectually honourable man who'd thought things through to their logical conclusion? And was there any difference?" (343) GOOD QUESTIONS I HAVE NO IDEA<br /><br />"He could have mentioned a change in Crake's fridge magnets." (347)<br /><br />And then there's the topic of the new creatures:<br /><br />"There's a distance, peaceful murmur from the village: human voices. If you can call them human. As long as they don't start singing. Their singing is unlike anything he ever heard in his vanished life: it's beyond the human level, or below it. As if crystals are singing; but not that, either. More like ferns unscrolling - something old, carboniferous, but at the same time newborn, fragrant, verdant. It reduces him, forces too many unwanted emotions upon him. He feels excluded, as if from a party which he will never be invited." (106)<br /><br />And then sometimes I just feel so badly for Jimmy/Snowman:<br /><br />"Sometimes he can conjure her up. At first she's pale and shadowy, but if he can say her name over and over, then maybe she'll glide into his body and be present with him in the flesh, and his hand on himself will become her hand...." (110)<br /><br />"In that village, she told him, some of the people could send their spirits out like that even before they were dead. It was well known. You could learn how to do it, the old women could teach you, and that way you could fly everywhere, you could see what was coming in the future, and send messages, and appear in other people's dreams." (124)<br />(I'm from this village!)<br /><br />[Oryx to Jimmy]"We should think only beautiful things, as much as we can. There is so much beautiful in the world if you look around. You are looking only at the dirt under your feet, Jimmy." (144)<br /><br />"He would never get used to her, she was fresh every time, she was a casketful of secrets. Any moment now she would open herself up, reveal to him the essential thing, the hidden thing at the core of life, or of her life, or of his life - the thing he was longing to know. The thing he'd always wanted. What would it be?"(314)<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/312707?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 01, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/30800-oriana" class="user" title="Oriana">Oriana</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <a href="/book/show/46756.Oryx_and_Crake" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780385721677" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/30800-oriana?shelf=read-2013" class="actionLinkLite">read-2013</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/30800-oriana?shelf=read-pre-goodreads" class="actionLinkLite">read-pre-goodreads</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer312707">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5609091322564532546">I just re-read this because despite a herculean goddamn effort, I have thus far failed to get my hands on <em>MaddAddam</em> and am having some kind of Margaret Atwood withdrawal. Seriously, WTF? What is the point of having spent a decade writhing around in the publishing world if I can't score the most sought-after proof of the moment? Fail, fail, fail. I almost bought it on eBay this morning—seriously, the ENTIRE INTERNET only had one copy for sale—but I got outbid at $40 and realized I needed to chill</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5609091322564532546">I just re-read this because despite a herculean goddamn effort, I have thus far failed to get my hands on <em>MaddAddam</em> and am having some kind of Margaret Atwood withdrawal. Seriously, WTF? What is the point of having spent a decade writhing around in the publishing world if I can't score the most sought-after proof of the moment? Fail, fail, fail. I almost bought it on eBay this morning—seriously, the ENTIRE INTERNET only had one copy for sale—but I got outbid at $40 and realized I needed to chill the fuck out. <br /><br />Uh, so this book rocks still, just like it did the first time. I am super incredibly in love with Margaret Atwood forever and ever amen; when I read <em>Cat's Eye</em> at sixteen it was as close as I've ever gotten to a transcendental experience. I like her realist books better than her dystopias, but good goddamn, no one does anything better than Margaret Atwood, and so when she does a dystopia, she motherfucking <em>does that shit</em>. <br /><br />I don't have anything else intelligent to say about this. Or, rather, I do; I'd like to meditate on soydines and how hard it was to have dinner with my vegan boyfriend while reading this, and about the ending and how it was kind of abrupt and I'm not sure I totally buy it, and about the way she structures all her books the same way—this like boomeranging back and forth from the present to the deep past, then present, then mid-past, then present, then recent past, until we're all caught up—and what is that called? And did she invent that? And is she just honestly the greatest writer of all time ever?<br /><br />But I don't have time for any of that now, sorry.</span>
